<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Major trauma</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"><span class="mw-redirectedfrom">  (Redirected from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/w/index.php?title=Physical_trauma&amp;redirect=no" title="Physical trauma">Physical trauma</a>)</span></div>

<div style="text-align: right; font-size:0.9em;">[<a class="extiw" href="https://www.wikidata.org/wiki/Q1064904" title="d:Q1064904">edit on Wikidata</a>]</div>

<p><b>Major trauma</b> is any <a href="/wiki/Injury" title="Injury">injury</a> that has the potential to cause prolonged <a href="/wiki/Disability" title="Disability">disability</a> or <a href="/wiki/Death" title="Death">death</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> There are many causes of major trauma, <a href="/wiki/Blunt_trauma" title="Blunt trauma">blunt</a> and <a href="/wiki/Penetrating_trauma" title="Penetrating trauma">penetrating</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Falling_(accident)" title="Falling (accident)">falls</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Motor_vehicle_collision" title="Motor vehicle collision">motor vehicle collisions</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stab_wound" title="Stab wound">stabbing wounds</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Gunshot_wound" title="Gunshot wound">gunshot wounds</a>. Depending on the severity of injury, quickness of management and transportation to an appropriate medical facility (called a <a href="/wiki/Trauma_center" title="Trauma center">trauma center</a>) may be necessary to prevent loss of life or limb. The initial assessment is critical, and involves a physical evaluation and also may include the use of imaging tools to determine the types of injuries accurately and to formulate a course of treatment.</p>
<p>In 2002, unintentional and intentional injuries were the fifth and seventh leading causes of deaths worldwide, accounting for 6.23% and 2.84% of all deaths. For research purposes the definition often is based on an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Injury_severity_score" title="Injury severity score">injury severity score</a> (ISS) of greater than 15.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">Classification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Major_trauma&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Classification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Injuries generally are classified by either severity, the location of damage, or a combination of both.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> Trauma also may be classified by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Demographics" title="Demographics">demographic group</a>, such as age or gender.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rosen2010_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rosen2010-4">[4]</a></sup> It also may be classified by the type of force applied to the body, such as <a href="/wiki/Blunt_trauma" title="Blunt trauma">blunt trauma</a> or <a href="/wiki/Penetrating_trauma" title="Penetrating trauma">penetrating trauma</a>. For research purposes injury may be classified using the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Barell_matrix&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Barell matrix (page does not exist)">Barell matrix</a>, which is based on <a href="/wiki/International_Statistical_Classification_of_Diseases_and_Related_Health_Problems#ICD-9-CM" title="International Statistical Classification of Diseases and Related Health Problems">ICD-9-CM</a>. The purpose of the matrix is for international standardization of the classification of trauma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> Major trauma sometimes is classified by body area; injuries affecting 40% are <a href="/wiki/Polytrauma" title="Polytrauma">polytrauma</a>, 30% <a href="/wiki/Head_injury" title="Head injury">head injuries</a>, 20% <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chest_trauma" title="Chest trauma">chest trauma</a>, 10%, <a href="/wiki/Abdominal_trauma" title="Abdominal trauma">abdominal trauma</a>, and 2%, <a href="/wiki/Limb_(anatomy)" title="Limb (anatomy)">extremity</a> trauma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rosen2010_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-Rosen2010-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ER2008_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-ER2008-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>Various scales exist to provide a quantifiable metric to measure the severity of injuries. The value may be used for <a href="/wiki/Triage" title="Triage">triaging</a> a patient or for statistical analysis. Injury scales measure damage to anatomical parts, physiological values (blood pressure etc.), <a href="/wiki/Comorbidity" title="Comorbidity">comorbidities</a>, or a combination of those. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abbreviated_injury_scale" title="Abbreviated injury scale">abbreviated injury scale</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glasgow_coma_scale" title="Glasgow coma scale">Glasgow coma scale</a> are used commonly to quantify injuries for the purpose of triaging and allow a system to monitor or "trend" a patient's condition in a clinical setting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> The data also may be used in epidemiological investigations and for research purposes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>Approximately 2% of those who have experienced significant trauma have a spinal cord injury.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ahn_1341.E2.80.9361_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ahn_1341.E2.80.9361-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Causes">Causes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Major_trauma&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Causes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Injuries may be caused by any combination of external forces that act physically against the body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> The leading causes of traumatic death are <a href="/wiki/Blunt_trauma" title="Blunt trauma">blunt trauma</a>, <a href="/wiki/Traffic_collision" title="Traffic collision">motor vehicle collisions</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Falling_(accident)" title="Falling (accident)">falls</a>, followed by <a href="/wiki/Penetrating_trauma" title="Penetrating trauma">penetrating trauma</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Stab_wound" title="Stab wound">stab wound</a>s or impaled objects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EMT-BMcGraw_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-EMT-BMcGraw-11">[11]</a></sup> Subsets of blunt trauma, are the number one and two causes of traumatic death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ec11_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-ec11-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>For statistical purposes, injuries are classified as either intentional such as suicide, or unintentional, such as a motor vehicle collision. Intentional injury is a common cause of traumas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-isbn0-340-92826-3_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-isbn0-340-92826-3-13">[13]</a></sup> Penetrating trauma is caused when a <a href="/wiki/Foreign_body" title="Foreign body">foreign body</a> such as a bullet or a knife enters the <a href="/wiki/Tissue_(biology)" title="Tissue (biology)">body tissue</a>, creating an open wound. In the United States, most deaths caused by penetrating trauma occur in urban areas and 80% of these deaths are caused by firearms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-isbn078173200x_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-isbn078173200x-14">[14]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Blast_injury" title="Blast injury">Blast injury</a> is a complex cause of trauma because it commonly includes both blunt and penetrating trauma, and also may be accompanied by a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Burn_injury" title="Burn injury">burn injury</a>. Trauma also may be associated with a particular activity, such as an <a href="/wiki/Occupational_injury" title="Occupational injury">occupational</a> or <a href="/wiki/Sports_injury" title="Sports injury">sports injury</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Pathophysiology">Pathophysiology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Major_trauma&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Pathophysiology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The body responds to traumatic injury both systemically and at the injury site.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-trauma_care_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-trauma_care-16">[16]</a></sup> This response attempts to protect vital organs such as the liver, to allow further cell duplication and to heal the damage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> The healing time of an injury depends on various factors including sex, age, and the severity of injury.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ICU_Book_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-ICU_Book-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<p>The symptoms of injury may manifest in many different ways, including:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Altered_mental_status" title="Altered mental status">Altered mental status</a></li>
<li>Fever</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Tachycardia" title="Tachycardia">Increased heart rate</a></li>
<li>Generalized <a href="/wiki/Edema" title="Edema">edema</a></li>
<li>Increased <a href="/wiki/Cardiac_output" title="Cardiac output">cardiac output</a></li>
<li>Increased rate of metabolism</li>
</ul>
<p>Various organ systems respond to injury to restore <a href="/wiki/Homeostasis" title="Homeostasis">homeostasis</a> by maintaining perfusion to the heart and brain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Inflammation" title="Inflammation">Inflammation</a> after injury occurs to protect against further damage and starts the healing process. Prolonged inflammation may cause <a href="/wiki/Multiple_organ_dysfunction_syndrome" title="Multiple organ dysfunction syndrome">multiple organ dysfunction syndrome</a> or <a href="/wiki/Systemic_inflammatory_response_syndrome" title="Systemic inflammatory response syndrome">systemic inflammatory response syndrome</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> Immediately after injury, the body increases production of glucose through <a href="/wiki/Gluconeogenesis" title="Gluconeogenesis">gluconeogenesis</a> and its consumption of fat via <a href="/wiki/Lipolysis" title="Lipolysis">lipolysis</a>. Next, the body tries to replenish its energy stores of glucose and protein via <a href="/wiki/Anabolism" title="Anabolism">anabolism</a>. In this state the body will temporarily increase its maximum expenditure for the purpose of healing injured cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ICU_Book_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-ICU_Book-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid15910820_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid15910820-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Diagnosis">Diagnosis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Major_trauma&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Diagnosis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Shotgun_wound-xray.JPG"><img alt="Radiograph of a close-range shotgun blast injury to the knee. Birdshot pellets are visible within and around the shattered patella, distal femur and proximal tibia." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2543" data-file-width="1961" height="285" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Shotgun_wound-xray.JPG/220px-Shotgun_wound-xray.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Shotgun_wound-xray.JPG/330px-Shotgun_wound-xray.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Shotgun_wound-xray.JPG/440px-Shotgun_wound-xray.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Shotgun_wound-xray.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Radiograph of a close-range <a href="/wiki/Shotgun" title="Shotgun">shotgun</a> <a href="/wiki/Blast_injury" title="Blast injury">blast injury</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Knee" title="Knee">knee</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Birdshot" title="Birdshot">Birdshot pellets</a> are visible within and around the shattered <a href="/wiki/Patella" title="Patella">patella</a>, distal <a href="/wiki/Femur" title="Femur">femur</a>, and proximal <a href="/wiki/Tibia" title="Tibia">tibia</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The initial assessment is critical in determining the extent of injuries and what will be needed to manage an injury, and for treating immediate life threats.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_examination">Physical examination</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Major_trauma&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Physical examination">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Primary <a href="/wiki/Physical_examination" title="Physical examination">physical examination</a> is undertaken to identify any life-threatening problems, after which the secondary examination is carried out. This may occur during transportation or upon arrival at the hospital. The secondary examination consists of a systematic assessment of the <a href="/wiki/Abdomen" title="Abdomen">abdominal</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_pelvis" title="Human pelvis">pelvic</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chest" title="Chest">thoracic</a> areas, a complete inspection of the body surface to find all injuries, and a <a href="/wiki/Neurological_examination" title="Neurological examination">neurological examination</a>. Injuries that may manifest themselves later, may be missed during the initial assessment, such as when a patient is brought into a hospital's emergency department.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ATLS2008_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-ATLS2008-23">[23]</a></sup> Generally, the physical examination is performed in a systematic way that first checks for any immediate life threats (primary survey), and then taking a more in depth examination (secondary survey).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Imaging">Imaging</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Major_trauma&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Imaging">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Persons with major trauma commonly have chest and pelvic <a href="/wiki/Radiography" title="Radiography">X-rays</a> taken,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ER2008_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-ER2008-6">[6]</a></sup> and, depending on the mechanism of injury and presentation, a <a href="/wiki/Focused_assessment_with_sonography_for_trauma" title="Focused assessment with sonography for trauma">focused assessment with sonography for trauma</a> (FAST) exam to check for internal bleeding. For those with relatively stable blood pressure, heart rate, and sufficient <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxygenation_(medical)" title="Oxygenation (medical)">oxygenation</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/X-ray_computed_tomography" title="X-ray computed tomography">CT scans</a> are useful.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ER2008_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-ER2008-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Trauma2010_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Trauma2010-25">[25]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Full-body_CT_scan" title="Full-body CT scan">Full-body CT scans</a>, known as pan-scans, improve the survival rate of those who have suffered major trauma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Huber2009_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Huber2009-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> These scans use intravenous injections for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radiocontrast" title="Radiocontrast">radiocontrast</a> agent, but not oral administration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Allen2004_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Allen2004-28">[28]</a></sup> There are concerns that intravenous contrast administration in trauma situations without confirming adequate renal function may cause damage to kidneys, but this does not appear to be significant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Trauma2010_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-Trauma2010-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the U.S., CT or <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_resonance_imaging" title="Magnetic resonance imaging">MRI</a> scans are performed on 15% of those with trauma in emergency rooms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Korley2010_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Korley2010-29">[29]</a></sup> Where blood pressure is low or the heart rate is increased—likely from bleeding in the abdomen—immediate surgery bypassing a CT scan is recommended.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> Modern 64-slice CT scans are able to rule out with a high degree of accuracy, significant injuries to the neck following blunt trauma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Surgical_techniques">Surgical techniques</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Major_trauma&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Surgical techniques">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Surgical techniques, using a tube or catheter to <a href="/wiki/Diagnostic_peritoneal_lavage" title="Diagnostic peritoneal lavage">drain fluid from the peritoneum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chest_tube" title="Chest tube">chest</a>, or the <a href="/wiki/Pericardiocentesis" title="Pericardiocentesis">pericardium around the heart</a>, often are used in cases of severe blunt trauma to the chest or abdomen, especially when a person is experiencing early signs of <a href="/wiki/Shock_(circulatory)" title="Shock (circulatory)">shock</a>. In those with <a href="/wiki/Hypotension" title="Hypotension">low blood-pressure</a>, likely because of bleeding in the abdominal cavity, <a href="/wiki/Laparotomy" title="Laparotomy">cutting through the abdominal wall surgically</a> is indicated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ER2008_6-3"><a href="#cite_note-ER2008-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prevention">Prevention</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Major_trauma&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Prevention">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Injury_prevention" title="Injury prevention">Injury prevention</a></div>
<p>By identifying risk factors present within a community and creating solutions to decrease the incidence of injury, trauma referral systems may help to enhance the overall health of a population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoyt2007_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hoyt2007-32">[32]</a></sup> Injury prevention strategies are commonly used to prevent injuries in children, who are a high risk population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-child_prevention_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-child_prevention-33">[33]</a></sup> Injury prevention strategies generally involve educating the general public about specific risk factors and developing strategies to avoid or reduce injuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cdcinjury_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-cdcinjury-34">[34]</a></sup> Legislation intended to prevent injury typically involves seatbelts, child car-seats, helmets, alcohol control, and increased enforcement of the legislation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreaves.2C_Porter.2C_Ryan.2C_Garner20086_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreaves.2C_Porter.2C_Ryan.2C_Garner20086-35">[35]</a></sup> Other controllable factors, such as the use of drugs including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alcoholic_beverage" title="Alcoholic beverage">alcohol</a> or <a href="/wiki/Cocaine" title="Cocaine">cocaine</a>, increases the risk of trauma by increasing the likelihood of traffic collisions, violence, and abuse occurring.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ER2008_6-4"><a href="#cite_note-ER2008-6">[6]</a></sup> Prescription drugs such as <a href="/wiki/Benzodiazepine" title="Benzodiazepine">benzodiazepines</a> may increase the risk of trauma in <a href="/wiki/Old_age" title="Old age">elderly</a> people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ER2008_6-5"><a href="#cite_note-ER2008-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>The care of acutely injured people in a public health system requires the involvement of bystanders, community members, health care professionals, and health care systems. It encompasses <a href="/wiki/Pre-hospital_trauma_assessment" title="Pre-hospital trauma assessment">pre-hospital trauma assessment</a> and care by <a href="/wiki/Emergency_medical_services" title="Emergency medical services">emergency medical services</a> personnel, emergency department assessment, treatment, stabilization, and in-hospital care among all age groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-CDC-36">[36]</a></sup> An established trauma system network is also an important component of community disaster preparedness, facilitating the care of people who have been involved in disasters that cause large numbers of casualties, such as earthquakes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoyt2007_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hoyt2007-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Management">Management</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Major_trauma&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Management">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:US_Navy_010531-N-3889M-004_Navy_Corpsman_Field_Training_Exercise.jpg"><img alt="Color photograph of a United States Navy hospital corpsman listening for correct placement of an endotracheal tube in a simulated trauma victim during a search and rescue exercise. <div class="thumb tright">

</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-hospital">Pre-hospital</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Major_trauma&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Pre-hospital">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The pre-hospital use of <a href="/wiki/Stabilization_(medicine)" title="Stabilization (medicine)">stabilization</a> techniques improves the chances of a person surviving the journey to the nearest trauma-equipped hospital. Emergency medicine services determines which people need treatment at a <a href="/wiki/Trauma_center" title="Trauma center">trauma center</a> as well as provide primary stabilization by checking and treating <a href="/wiki/ABC_(medicine)" title="ABC (medicine)">airway, breathing, and circulation</a> as well as assessing for disability and gaining exposure to check for other injuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ATLS2008_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-ATLS2008-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spinal_immobilization" title="Spinal immobilization">Spinal motion restriction</a> by securing the neck with a <a href="/wiki/Cervical_collar" title="Cervical collar">cervical collar</a> and placing the person on a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Long_spine_board" title="Long spine board">long spine board</a> was of high importance in the pre-hospital setting, but due to lack of evidence to support its use, the practice is losing favor. Instead, it is recommend that more exclusive criteria be met such as age and neurological deficits to indicate the need of these adjuncts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> This may be accomplished with other medical transport devices such as a <a href="/wiki/Kendrick_Extrication_Device" title="Kendrick Extrication Device">Kendrick extrication device</a>, before moving the person.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Karbi1988_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Karbi1988-39">[39]</a></sup> It is important to quickly control severe bleeding with direct pressure to the wound and consider the use of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hemostatic_agents" title="Hemostatic agents">hemostatic agents</a> or <a href="/wiki/Tourniquet" title="Tourniquet">tourniquets</a> if the bleeding continues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moore2013p154_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Moore2013p154-40">[40]</a></sup> Conditions such as impending airway obstruction, enlargening neck hematoma, or unconsciousness require intubation. It is unclear, however, if this is best performed before reaching hospital or in the hospital.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bulger2007_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bulger2007-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<p>Rapid transportation of severely injured patients improves the outcome in trauma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ER2008_6-6"><a href="#cite_note-ER2008-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ATLS2008_23-2"><a href="#cite_note-ATLS2008-23">[23]</a></sup> Helicopter EMS transport reduces mortality compared to ground-based transport in adult trauma patients.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> Before arrival at the hospital, the availability of <a href="/wiki/Advanced_life_support" title="Advanced life support">advanced life support</a> does not greatly improve the outcome for major trauma when compared to the administration of <a href="/wiki/Basic_life_support" title="Basic life support">basic life support</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stiell2008_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stiell2008-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Liberman2007_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Liberman2007-44">[44]</a></sup> Evidence is inconclusive in determining support for pre-hospital <a href="/wiki/Intravenous_therapy" title="Intravenous therapy">intravenous fluid resuscitation</a> while some evidence has found it may be harmful.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dretzke2004_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dretzke2004-45">[45]</a></sup> Hospitals with designated trauma centers have improved outcomes when compared to hospitals without them,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ER2008_6-7"><a href="#cite_note-ER2008-6">[6]</a></sup> and outcomes may improve when persons who have experienced trauma are transferred directly to a trauma center.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nirula2010_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nirula2010-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In-hospital">In-hospital</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Major_trauma&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: In-hospital">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Management of those with trauma often requires the help of many healthcare specialists including physicians, nurses, respiratory therapists, and social workers. Cooperation allows many actions to be completed at once. Generally the first step of managing trauma is to perform a primary survey that evaluates a person's airway, breathing, circulation, and neurologic status.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> After immediate life threats are controlled, a person is either moved into an operating room for immediate surgical correction of the injuries, or a secondary survey is performed that is a more detailed head-to-toe assessment of the person.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<p>Indications for intubation include airway obstruction, inability to protect the airway, and respiratory failure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> Examples of these indications include penetrating neck trauma, expanding neck hematoma, and being unconscious, among other indications. In general, the method of intubation used is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rapid_sequence_intubation" title="Rapid sequence intubation">rapid sequence intubation</a> followed by ventilation. Assessment of circulation in those with trauma includes control of active bleeding. When a person is first brought in, vital signs are checked, an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ECG" title="ECG">ECG</a> is performed, and, if needed, vascular access is obtained. Other tests should be performed to get a baseline measurement of their current blood chemistry, such as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arterial_blood_gas" title="Arterial blood gas">arterial blood gas</a> or <a href="/wiki/Thromboelastography" title="Thromboelastography">thromboelastography</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> In those with <a href="/wiki/Cardiac_arrest" title="Cardiac arrest">cardiac arrest</a> due to trauma chest compressions are considered futile, but still recommended.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AHAPart12_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-AHAPart12-51">[51]</a></sup> Correcting the underlying cause such as a <a href="/wiki/Pneumothorax" title="Pneumothorax">pneumothorax</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pericardial_tamponade" title="Pericardial tamponade">pericardial tamponade</a>, if present, may help.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AHAPart12_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-AHAPart12-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Focused_assessment_with_sonography_for_trauma" title="Focused assessment with sonography for trauma">FAST</a> exam may help assess for internal bleeding. In certain traumas, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maxillofacial" title="Maxillofacial">maxillofacial</a> trauma, it may be beneficial to have a highly trained health care provider available to maintain airway, breathing, and circulation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Maxillofacial_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Maxillofacial-52">[52]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Intravenous_fluids">Intravenous fluids</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Major_trauma&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Intravenous fluids">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Traditionally, high-volume <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intravenous_fluids" title="Intravenous fluids">intravenous fluids</a> were given to people who had poor perfusion due to trauma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EMB11_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-EMB11-53">[53]</a></sup> This is still appropriate in cases with isolated extremity trauma, thermal trauma, or head injuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-COCC2010_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-COCC2010-54">[54]</a></sup> In general, however, giving lots of fluids appears to increase the risk of death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> Current evidence supports limiting the use of fluids for penetrating thorax and abdominal injuries, allowing mild hypotension to persist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rosen2010_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-Rosen2010-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-COCC2010_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-COCC2010-54">[54]</a></sup> Targets include a <a href="/wiki/Mean_arterial_pressure" title="Mean arterial pressure">mean arterial pressure</a> of 60 mmHg, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Systolic_blood_pressure" title="Systolic blood pressure">systolic blood pressure</a> of 70–90 mmHg,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EMB11_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-EMB11-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tint10_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tint10-56">[56]</a></sup> or the re-establishment of peripheral pulses and adequate ability to think.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EMB11_53-2"><a href="#cite_note-EMB11-53">[53]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hypertonic_saline" title="Hypertonic saline">Hypertonic saline</a> has been studied and found to be of little difference from normal saline.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kob2012_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kob2012-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<p>As no intravenous fluids used for initial resuscitation have been shown to be superior, warmed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lactated_Ringer%27s_solution" title="Lactated Ringer's solution">Lactated Ringer's solution</a> continues to be the solution of choice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EMB11_53-3"><a href="#cite_note-EMB11-53">[53]</a></sup> If blood products are needed, a greater use of <a href="/wiki/Fresh_frozen_plasma" title="Fresh frozen plasma">fresh frozen plasma</a> and <a href="/wiki/Platelet" title="Platelet">platelets</a> relative to <a href="/wiki/Packed_red_blood_cells" title="Packed red blood cells">packed red blood cells</a> has been found to improve survival and lower overall blood product use;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greer2010_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Greer2010-58">[58]</a></sup> a ratio of 1:1:1 is recommended.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tint10_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tint10-56">[56]</a></sup> The success of platelets has been attributed to the fact that they may prevent <a href="/wiki/Coagulopathy" title="Coagulopathy">coagulopathy</a> from developing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wsj_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-wsj-59">[59]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cell_salvage_and_autotransfusion" title="Cell salvage and autotransfusion">Cell salvage and autotransfusion</a> also may be used.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EMB11_53-4"><a href="#cite_note-EMB11-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blood_substitutes" title="Blood substitutes">Blood substitutes</a> such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hemoglobin-based_oxygen_carriers" title="Hemoglobin-based oxygen carriers">hemoglobin-based oxygen carriers</a> are in development; however, as of 2013 there are none available for commercial use in North America or Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EMB11_53-5"><a href="#cite_note-EMB11-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UPT2008_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-UPT2008-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spahn2005_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Spahn2005-61">[61]</a></sup> These products are only available for general use in South Africa and Russia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UPT2008_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-UPT2008-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medications">Medications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Major_trauma&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Medications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Tranexamic_acid" title="Tranexamic acid">Tranexamic acid</a> decreases the mortality rate in people who are bleeding due to trauma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CRASH2010_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-CRASH2010-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> It only appears to be beneficial, however, if administered within the first three hours after trauma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> For severe bleeding, for example from <a href="/wiki/Coagulopathy" title="Coagulopathy">bleeding disorders</a>, <a href="/wiki/Recombinant_factor_VIIa" title="Recombinant factor VIIa">recombinant factor VIIa</a>—a protein that assists blood clotting—may be appropriate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ER2008_6-8"><a href="#cite_note-ER2008-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-COCC2010_54-2"><a href="#cite_note-COCC2010-54">[54]</a></sup> While it decreases blood use, it does not appear to decrease the mortality rate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> In those without previous factor VII deficiency, its use is not recommended outside of trial situations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<p>Other medications may be used in conjunction with other procedures to stabilize a person who has sustained a significant injury.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rosen2010_4-3"><a href="#cite_note-Rosen2010-4">[4]</a></sup> While positive <a href="/wiki/Inotrope" title="Inotrope">inotropic medications</a> such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Norepinephrine_(drug)" title="Norepinephrine (drug)">norepinephrine</a> sometimes are used in hemorrhagic shock as a result of trauma, there is a lack of evidence for their use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> Therefore, as of 2012 they have not been recommended.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kob2012_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kob2012-57">[57]</a></sup> Allowing a low blood pressure may be preferred in some situations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Surgery">Surgery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Major_trauma&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Surgery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The decision whether to perform surgery is determined by the extent of the damage and the anatomical location of the injury. Bleeding must be controlled before definitive repair may occur.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Damage_control_surgery" title="Damage control surgery">Damage control surgery</a> is used to manage severe trauma in which there is a <a href="/wiki/Trauma_triad_of_death" title="Trauma triad of death">cycle</a> of <a href="/wiki/Metabolic_acidosis" title="Metabolic acidosis">metabolic acidosis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hypothermia" title="Hypothermia">hypothermia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hypotension" title="Hypotension">hypotension</a> that may lead to death, if not corrected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ER2008_6-9"><a href="#cite_note-ER2008-6">[6]</a></sup> The main principal of the procedure involves performing the least number of procedures to save life and limb; less critical procedures are left until the victim is more stable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ER2008_6-10"><a href="#cite_note-ER2008-6">[6]</a></sup> Approximately 15% of all people with trauma have abdominal injuries, and approximately 25% of these require exploratory surgery. The majority of preventable deaths from trauma result from unrecognised intra-abdominal bleeding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Management_of_Abdominal_Trauma_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Management_of_Abdominal_Trauma-70">[70]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prognosis">Prognosis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Major_trauma&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Prognosis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Trauma deaths occur in immediate, early, or late stages. Immediate deaths usually are due to <a href="/wiki/Apnea" title="Apnea">apnea</a>, severe brain or high spinal cord injury, or rupture of the heart or of large blood vessels. Early deaths occur within minutes to hours and often are due to <a href="/wiki/Subdural_hematoma" title="Subdural hematoma">hemorrhages in the outer meningeal layer of the brain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Epidural_hematoma" title="Epidural hematoma">torn arteries</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hemothorax" title="Hemothorax">blood around the lungs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pneumothorax" title="Pneumothorax">air around the lungs</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ruptured_spleen" title="Ruptured spleen">ruptured spleen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Abdominal_trauma#Liver" title="Abdominal trauma">liver laceration</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Pelvic_fracture" title="Pelvic fracture">pelvic fracture</a>. Immediate access to care may be crucial to prevent death in persons experiencing major trauma. Late deaths occurs days or weeks after the injury<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ATLS2008_23-3"><a href="#cite_note-ATLS2008-23">[23]</a></sup> and often are related to infection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> Prognosis is better in countries with a dedicated trauma system where injured persons are provided quick and effective access to proper treatment facilities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ER2008_6-11"><a href="#cite_note-ER2008-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>Long-term prognosis frequently is complicated by pain; more than half of trauma patients have moderate to severe pain one year after injury.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rivara2008_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rivara2008-72">[72]</a></sup> Many also experience a reduced <a href="/wiki/Quality_of_life" title="Quality of life">quality of life</a> years after an injury,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ulvik2008_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ulvik2008-73">[73]</a></sup> with 20% of victims sustaining some form of disability.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peitzman2008_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-Peitzman2008-74">[74]</a></sup> Physical trauma may lead to development of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Post-traumatic_stress_disorder" title="Post-traumatic stress disorder">post-traumatic stress disorder</a> (PTSD).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DSM_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-DSM-75">[75]</a></sup> One study has found no correlation between the severity of trauma and the development of PTSD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Feinstein1991_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-Feinstein1991-76">[76]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Epidemiology">Epidemiology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Major_trauma&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Epidemiology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_preventable_causes_of_death" title="List of preventable causes of death">List of preventable causes of death</a></div>

<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Accidents.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="300" data-file-width="450" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Accidents.svg/220px-Accidents.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Accidents.svg/330px-Accidents.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Accidents.svg/440px-Accidents.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Accidents.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Incidence of accidents by activity in Denmark</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Trauma is the sixth leading cause of death worldwide, resulting in five million or 10% of all deaths annually.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Epi09_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Epi09-78">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oxford2010_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-Oxford2010-79">[79]</a></sup> It is the fifth leading cause of significant disability.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Epi09_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-Epi09-78">[78]</a></sup> About half of trauma deaths are in people aged between 15 and 45 years and trauma is the leading cause of death in this age group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oxford2010_79-1"><a href="#cite_note-Oxford2010-79">[79]</a></sup> Injury affects more males; 68% of injuries occur in males<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2013nejm_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-2013nejm-80">[80]</a></sup> and death from trauma is twice as common in males as it is in females, this is believed to be because males are much more willing to engage in risk-taking activities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oxford2010_79-2"><a href="#cite_note-Oxford2010-79">[79]</a></sup> Teenagers and young adults are more likely to need hospitalization from injuries than other age groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-p23_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-p23-81">[81]</a></sup> While elderly persons are less likely to be injured, they are more likely to die from injuries sustained due to various physiological differences that make it more difficult for the body to compensate for the injuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-p23_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-p23-81">[81]</a></sup> The primary causes of traumatic death are central nervous system injuries and <a href="/wiki/Exsanguination" title="Exsanguination">substantial blood loss</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Epi09_78-2"><a href="#cite_note-Epi09-78">[78]</a></sup> Various classification scales exist for use with trauma to determine the severity of injuries, which are used to determine the resources used and, for statistical collection.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Major_trauma&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The human remains discovered at the site of <a href="/wiki/Nataruk" title="Nataruk">Nataruk</a> in <a href="/wiki/Turkana_County" title="Turkana County">Turkana</a>, Kenya, are claimed to show major trauma, blunt and penetrating trauma, caused by violent trauma to the head, neck, ribs, knees, and hands that has been interpreted by some researchers as establishing the existence of warfare between two groups of hunter-gatherers 10,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> The evidence for blunt force trauma at Nataruk has been challenged, however, and the interpretation that the site represents an early example of warfare has been questioned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Society_and_culture">Society and culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Major_trauma&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Society and culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Economics">Economics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Major_trauma&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Economics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The financial cost of trauma includes both the amount of money spent on treatment and the loss of potential economic gain through absence from work. The average financial cost for the treatment of traumatic injury in the United States is approximately <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$334,000</span> per person, making it costlier than the treatment of cancer and <a href="/wiki/Cardiovascular_disease" title="Cardiovascular disease">cardiovascular diseases</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PHTLS_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-PHTLS-84">[84]</a></sup> One reason for the high cost of the treatment for trauma is the increased possibility of complications, which leads to the need for more interventions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid18656640_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid18656640-85">[85]</a></sup> Maintaining a trauma center is costly because they are open continuously and maintain a state of readiness to receive patients, even if there are none.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid14706578_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid14706578-86">[86]</a></sup> In addition to the direct costs of the treatment, there also is a burden on the economy due to lost wages and productivity, which in 2009, accounted for approximately <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$693.5 billion</span> in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Low-_and_middle-income_countries">Low- and middle-income countries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Major_trauma&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Low- and middle-income countries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Citizens of <a href="/wiki/Developing_country" title="Developing country">low-</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Middle_income_country" title="Middle income country">middle-income</a> countries (LMICs) often have higher mortality rates from injury. These countries accounted for 89% of all deaths from injury worldwide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2013nejm_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-2013nejm-80">[80]</a></sup> Many of these countries do not have access to sufficient surgical care and many do not have a trauma system in place. In addition, most LMICs do not have a pre-hospital care system that treats injured persons initially and transports them to hospital quickly, resulting in most casualty paients being transported by private vehicles. Also, their hospitals lack the appropriate equipment, organizational resources, or trained staff.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-care_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-care-88">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-strength_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-strength-89">[89]</a></sup> By 2020, the amount of trauma-related deaths is expected to decline in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High_income_country" title="High income country">high-income countries</a>, while in low- to middle-income countries it is expected to increase.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreaves.2C_Porter.2C_Ryan.2C_Garner20082_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreaves.2C_Porter.2C_Ryan.2C_Garner20082-90">[90]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Special_populations">Special populations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Major_trauma&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Special populations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Children">Children</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Major_trauma&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Children">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Pediatric_trauma" title="Pediatric trauma">Pediatric trauma</a></div>
<table class="wikitable" style="float: right; margin-left:15px; text-align:center">
<tr>
<th>Cause</th>
<th>Deaths per year</th>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Traffic_collision" title="Traffic collision">Traffic collision</a></td>
<td>
<p>260,000</p>
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Drowning" title="Drowning">Drowning</a></td>
<td>
<p>175,000</p>
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Burn" title="Burn">Burns</a></td>
<td>
<p>96,000</p>
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Falling_(accident)" title="Falling (accident)">Falls</a></td>
<td>
<p>47,000</p>
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Toxin" title="Toxin">Toxins</a></td>
<td>
<p>45,000</p>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>Due to anatomical and physiological differences, injuries in children need to be approached differently from those in adults.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dickinson2008_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dickinson2008-91">[91]</a></sup> Accidents are the leading cause of death in children between 1 and 14 years old.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peitzman2008_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-Peitzman2008-74">[74]</a></sup> In the United States, approximately sixteen million children go to an emergency department due to some form of injury every year,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peitzman2008_74-2"><a href="#cite_note-Peitzman2008-74">[74]</a></sup> with boys being more frequently injured than girls by a ratio of 2:1.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peitzman2008_74-3"><a href="#cite_note-Peitzman2008-74">[74]</a></sup> The world's five most common unintentional injuries in children as of 2008 are road crashes, drowning, burns, falls, and poisoning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-92">[92]</a></sup></p>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Body_weight#Estimation_in_children" title="Body weight">Weight estimation</a> is an important part of managing trauma in children because the accurate dosing of medicine may be critical for resuscitative efforts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid20825816_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid20825816-93">[93]</a></sup> A number of methods to estimate weight, including the <a href="/wiki/Broselow_tape" title="Broselow tape">Broselow tape</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leffler_formula" title="Leffler formula">Leffler formula</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theron_formula" title="Theron formula">Theron formula</a> exist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ped09_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ped09-94">[94]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pregnancy">Pregnancy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Major_trauma&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Pregnancy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Trauma occurs in approximately 5% of all pregnancies,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EMP2008_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-EMP2008-95">[95]</a></sup> and is the leading cause of maternal death. Additionally, pregnant women may experience <a href="/wiki/Placental_abruption" title="Placental abruption">placental abruption</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Premature_birth" title="Premature birth">pre-term labor</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Uterine_rupture" title="Uterine rupture">uterine rupture</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EMP2008_95-1"><a href="#cite_note-EMP2008-95">[95]</a></sup> There are diagnostic issues during pregnancy; <a href="/wiki/Ionizing_radiation" title="Ionizing radiation">ionizing radiation</a> has been shown to cause birth defects,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rosen2010_4-4"><a href="#cite_note-Rosen2010-4">[4]</a></sup> although the doses used for typical exams generally are considered safe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EMP2008_95-2"><a href="#cite_note-EMP2008-95">[95]</a></sup> Due to normal <a href="/wiki/Maternal_physiological_changes_in_pregnancy" title="Maternal physiological changes in pregnancy">physiological changes that occur during pregnancy</a>, shock may be more difficult to diagnose.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rosen2010_4-5"><a href="#cite_note-Rosen2010-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BTLS_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-BTLS-96">[96]</a></sup> Where the woman is more than 23 weeks pregnant, it is recommended that the fetus be monitored for at least four hours by <a href="/wiki/Cardiotocography" title="Cardiotocography">cardiotocography</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EMP2008_95-3"><a href="#cite_note-EMP2008-95">[95]</a></sup></p>
<p>A number of treatments beyond typical trauma care may be needed when the patient is pregnant. Because the weight of the uterus on the <a href="/wiki/Inferior_vena_cava" title="Inferior vena cava">inferior vena cava</a> may decrease blood return to the heart, it may be very beneficial to lay a women in late pregnancy on her left side.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EMP2008_95-4"><a href="#cite_note-EMP2008-95">[95]</a></sup> also recommended are <a href="/wiki/Rho(D)_immune_globulin" title="Rho(D) immune globulin">Rho(D) immune globulin</a> in those who are rh negative, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Corticosteroids" title="Corticosteroids">corticosteroids</a> in those who are 24 to 34 weeks and may need delivery or a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caesarian_section" title="Caesarian section">caesarian section</a> in the event of cardiac arrest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EMP2008_95-5"><a href="#cite_note-EMP2008-95">[95]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Research">Research</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Major_trauma&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Research">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Traumatology" title="Traumatology">Traumatology</a></div>
<p>Most research on trauma occurs during war and military conflicts as militaries will increase trauma research spending in order to prevent combat related deaths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid18483051_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid18483051-97">[97]</a></sup> Some research is being conducted on patients who were admitted into an <a href="/wiki/Intensive_care_unit" title="Intensive care unit">intensive care unit</a> or trauma center, and received a trauma diagnosis that caused a negative change in their health-related quality of life, with a potential to create anxiety and symptoms of depression.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid19088550_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid19088550-98">[98]</a></sup> New preserved blood products also are being researched for use in pre-hospital care; it is impractical to use the currently available blood products in a timely fashion in remote, rural settings or in theaters of war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid21718901_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid21718901-99">[99]</a></sup></p>
